StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat Biotechnol. Nature Publishing Group, a division of Macmillan Publishers Limited. All Rights Reserved. 33, 290–5 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1852">56</a></sup>, setting the minimum junction coverage to 5 (option –j). The transcripts were further assembled using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Open Access Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced. Genome Biol. 9 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1856">57</a></sup>, a eukaryotic genome annotation tool that exploits spliced alignments of expressed transcript sequences to automatically model gene structures.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene prediction</h3><p>Gene prediction resulted integrating several sources of evidence: (i) RNA-seq data; (ii) nucleotide and protein alignments; (iii) <i>de novo</i> gene training and prediction. Five different programs were used for <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction: Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 465–7 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1874">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stanke, M., Schöffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 11, 1–11 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1877">59</a></sup>, Snap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 1–9 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1881">60</a></sup>, Glimmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–9 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1885">61</a></sup> and GeneMark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Lomsadze, A., Burns, P. D. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Integration of mapped RNA-Seq reads into automatic training of eukaryotic gene finding algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, 1–8 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1890">62</a></sup>. Intron coordinates derived from RNA-Seq read alignments were provided to GeneMark. Gene models generated by PASA were used to train Snap, Glimmer and Augustus. Briefly, the PASA alignment assemblies were used to automatically extract protein coding regions in order to generate a high-quality data set for training <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors. To discard the lowest quality gene models generated by PASA, from the training dataset, only complete genes and validated through a similarity search with blast against a dataset of <i>C. canephora</i> were considered. From the blast search, only proteins with a match with an e-value lower that 1e-<sup>30</sup> and an alignment coverage higher than 90% were used to train the <i>ab initio</i> predictors.</p><p>Nucleotide and protein sequences ranging from close to distantly related organisms belonging to eudycotyledon taxonomic rank, Gentianales order, Coffea genus and <i>C. canephora</i> species were downloaded from NCBI and aligned to the reference genome using exonerate (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/about/vertebrate-genomics/software/exonerate">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/about/vertebrate-genomics/software/exonerate</a>). Only high-quality alignments were retained applying the following stringent criteria: 30% identity and 70% alignment coverage at the protein level, 50% identity and 70% alignment coverage at the nucleotide level.</p><p>Previously collected evidence was combined for gene prediction using EVidenceModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Open Access Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced. Genome Biol. 9 (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1918">57</a></sup> in order to obtain a single gene model. EVidenceModeler (EVM) combines <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions and protein and transcript alignments into weighted consensus gene structures. To reduce false positive prediction and improve the overall gene prediction quality, several filters were applied:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none"><li><span class="u-custom-list-number">1.</span><p>Genes predicted only by <i>ab initio</i> programs were considered only if they were confirmed by at least two different <i>ab initio</i> programs, if they were complete (with a start and a stop codon) and longer than 300 base pairs.</p></li><li><span class="u-custom-list-number">2.</span><p>Gene supported by external evidence (<i>e.g</i>. proteins/RNA-seq) were considered if confirmed by at least two different types of evidence or by one external evidence and one <i>ab initio</i> gene predictor.</p></li><li><span class="u-custom-list-number">3.</span><p>Predicted genes with a low <i>ab initio</i> support (as per step 1) were further processed. Those supported by only one <i>ab initio</i> program were retained only if found in a database of Coffea protein sequences. While proteins with a sequence coverage match higher than 50% for both query and subject and an e-value lower than 1e-<sup>6</sup> were recovered.</p></li></ol><p>Gene models passing these filters were further processed using PASA to add UTR regions and predict alternative splicing.</p><p>To remove multiple isoforms of the same gene and sequence redundancy, DNA coding sequences were clustered using CD-HIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658–9 (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1968">63</a></sup> with option -g set to 1 and for each gene locus we selected the longest transcripts isoform. CD-HIT was initially run setting the clustering percentage identity to 0.9. A Perl script was developed to parse the output in order to empirically identify a threshold that clustered as many genes as possible while keeping subgenome k-mer classified genes in proper clusters. The optimal clustering percentage identity was obtained at 0.9961 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene Annotation</h3><p>BLASTp similarity searches (e-value threshold of 1e<sup>−5</sup>) of <i>C. arabica</i> predicted genes were performed against the non-redundant protein database (NCBI). InterProscan5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Jones, P. et al. Sequence analysis InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–40 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1988">64</a></sup> was used to obtain the conserved protein domains and functional annotation. The databases used included PROSITE patterns, PRINTS, PFAM, PRODOM, SMART, TIGRFAM, and PANTHER. Gene Ontology and KEGG classifications were predicted running BLAST2GO 2.6.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–6 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d29985e1992">65</a></sup> on the BLASTp and InterProscan outputs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant material for genotyping</h3><p>An extensive number of accessions from <i>C. arabica</i> (781), <i>C. canephora</i> (35) and <i>C. eugenioides</i> (10) were collected for genotypic analysis for this study. Because 45 of the <i>Coffea arabica</i> accessions failed to produce sufficient reads following GBS analysis they were removed from the study leaving a total of 736 Arabica accessions. The final <i>Coffea arabica</i> genotypes included in this study were represented as follows: 648 <i>C. arabica</i> accessions provided by CATIE and 88 <i>C. arabica</i> accessions collected in Yemen provided by Sana’a University. The <i>C. canephora</i> and <i>C. eugenioides</i> accessions were collected by IRD (Institut de Recherche pour le Développement, France)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Anthony, F., Berthaud, J., Guillaumet, J. L. &amp; Lourd, M. Collecting wild coffea species in Kenya and Tanzania. Plant Genet Ressources Newsl. 69, 23–9 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2032">66</a></sup> and provided by CATIE or CIRAD. The list of all 781 accessions is provided in Dataset S2.</p><p>In relation to this history, the Arabica accessions are coded using the following categories:</p><p>Survey Ethiopia: 441 accessions from the FAO survey in Ethiopia (FAO 1964), 84 from the ORSTOM survey (1966) in Ethiopia (Guillaumet 1967) and 16 accessions from the ‘Lejeune survey’ or other surveys collected in Ethiopia before 1957. In Dataset S2 geographic coordinates and altitude for 359 accessions provided by botanists during the FAO and ORSTOM surveys is provided.</p><p>Landrace cultivated: 49 accessions of Ethiopian cultivated populations that have been collected in Ethiopian farms in addition to the FAO and ORSTOM surveys. These surveys are less documented than the FAO and ORSTOM surveys.</p><p>Survey Yemen: 93 accessions representing subspontaneous-derived accessions cultivated in Yemen and collected by Sana’a University (88 accessions) or by FAO (5 accessions planted in the CATIE collection). Those accessions can be considered as domesticated. For 28 accessions provided by Sana’a University, the altitude and the geographical coordinates are reported in Dataset S2.</p><p>East Africa and Indian Old varieties: 45 accessions of varieties selected in the 30’s in India and East Africa.</p><p>Typica/Bourbon cultivars: Seven accessions from the CATIE field GeneBank that conformed to the botanical varieties described by Krug and Carvalho (1951). In this study they represent the two widely cultivated varieties in Asia and Latin America for more than two centuries.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">DNA extraction and genotyping</h3><p>Leaves were collected and lyophilized, and genomic DNA was extracted using the ADNid method (<a href="http://www.adnid.fr/index-2-4A.html">http://www.adnid.fr/index-2-4A.html</a>) at ADNid (Montpellier, France).</p><p>Genotyping by sequencing (GBS) was conducted at the Cornell University Institute for Genomic Diversity (<a href="http://www.igd.cornell.edu/index.cfm/page/GBS.htm">http://www.igd.cornell.edu/index.cfm/page/GBS.htm</a>). Illumina template libraries were produced using the restriction enzyme <i>Pst</i>I followed by single-end sequencing on a HiSeq2000 (Illumina) as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Elshire, R. J. et al. A Robust, Simple Genotyping-by-Sequencing (GBS) Approach for High Diversity Species. PLoS One 6, 1–10 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2077">67</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">GBS analysis</h3><p>787 <i>C. arabica</i> varieties along with 10 <i>C. eugenioides</i> and 35 <i>C. canephora</i> accessions were subjected to GBS using the restriction enzyme <i>Pst</i>I<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Elshire, R. J. et al. A Robust, Simple Genotyping-by-Sequencing (GBS) Approach for High Diversity Species. PLoS One 6, 1–10 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2101">67</a></sup>. Ninety-six barcoded accessions were pooled, and each pool was run in one lane of a flow cell on an Illumina HiSeq2000 using a 91 bp single-end sequencing mode at the Institute for Genomic Diversity (IGD) at Cornell University. In total ~172 GB of DNA sequence data was obtained. Reads were initially processed to remove the individual barcodes corresponding to each accession and separated into individual fastq files for each accession using a custom python script. To avoid false positive SNP detection due to the allopolyploid nature of the <i>C. arabica</i> genome (<i>i.e</i>. polymorphism between the two subgenomes), two <i>in silico</i> reference sequences were generated, one for each of the two subgenomes. The canephora <i>in silico</i> reference was composed by (i) the assembled canephora subgenome, (ii) a full homoeologous complement of the assembled eugenioides subgenome, and (iii) the unassigned scaffolds. Similarly, the eugenioides <i>in silico</i> reference was composed by (i) the assembled eugenioides subgenome, (ii) a full homoeologous complement of the assembled canephora subgenome, and (iii) the unassigned scaffolds. In order to produce the full homoeologous complement of the assembled canephora subgenome, WGS reads of <i>C. arabica</i> ‘Bourbon Vermelho’ were aligned using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–60 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2125">68</a></sup> against the assembled canephora subgenome and the unassigned scaffolds. Then, homoeologous SNPs were called using GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2129">69</a></sup> with default parameters and the alternative homoeologous reference was generated using GATK FastaAlternateReferenceMaker for sites with minimum depth 50 and allele frequency between 0.25 and 0.75. The same procedure was applied to obtain the full homoeologous complement of the assembled eugenioides subgenome.</p><p>GBS reads were aligned using BWA-MEM v0.7.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–60 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2136">68</a></sup> against the canephora and eugenioides <i>in silico</i> references in order to call allelic SNPs in the two subgenomes, respectively. For both artificial references, SNP calling was performed using Stacks v2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Catchen, J., Hohenlohe, P. A., Bassham, S., Amores, A. &amp; Cresko, W. A. Stacks: an analysis tool set for population genomics. Mol Ecol. 22, 3124–40 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2143">70</a></sup>. Only variant sites covered by at least 10 reads were retained. Heterozygous SNPs with an allele frequency lower than 0.25 or higher than 0.75 were discarded. Polymorphisms detected using the two artificial references were merged and only variant sites called in native scaffolds of each subgenome were retained for subsequent analyses. To obtain a dataset of SNPs for STRUCTURE and PC analyses, Stacks was run with the option –r (minimum percentage of individuals in a population required to process a locus for that population) set to 0.75 and the option–max-clipped (maximum soft-clipping level, in fraction of read length) set to the default value of 0.20, corresponding to a minimum stack length of 73 bp. Variants sites with seven or eight missing genotype calls out of the eight Bourbon/Typica accessions were interpreted as misalignments and filtered out.</p><p>Principal Component Analysis was performed using the R package ade4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Dray, S. &amp; Dufour, A. The ade4 Package: Implementing the Duality Diagram for Ecologists. J. Stat Softw. 22 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2150">71</a></sup>. A hierarchical study of the diversity has been conducted using a model-based clustering procedure with admixture as implemented in STRUCTURE v2.3.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Pritchard, J. K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genet Soc Am. 155, 945–59 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2154">72</a></sup>. Runs were performed at values of <i>k</i> ranging from 1 to 13 with 10 replicates per K and a burn-in period of 75,000 and 75,000 MCMC repetitions. Plotting <i>k</i> vs ΔK indicated that the highest value was for 2 groups followed by 3 groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#MOESM1">S2</a>). Hence, after applying a threshold of 0.80 of membership, STRUCTURE was performed again on each of the 2 populations. According to ΔK, the second population was divided in 2 groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#MOESM1">S2</a>). A Principal Coordinates Analysis (PCoA) was performed in R (version 3.5.1, 64 bit; <a href="http://www.r-project.org/">www.r-project.org/</a>) with ape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Paradis, E., Claude, J. &amp; Strimmer, K. APE: Analyses of phylogenetics and evolution in R language. Bioinformatics 20, 289–90 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2175">73</a></sup>, using provesti distance matrix calculated using poppr package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Kamvar, Z. N., Tabima, J. F. &amp; Grünwald, N. J. Poppr: an R package for genetic analysis of populations with clonal, partially clonal, and/or sexual reproduction. PeerJ. 2 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2179">74</a></sup>. The PCoA was plotted with ggplot2 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>Given the low level of diversity of the species and the high frequency of the <i>PstI</i> restriction sites, we increased genome sampling for a better estimation of π, Tajima’s <i>D</i> and private or shared SNPs in <i>C. arabica</i>, <i>C. canephora</i>, and <i>C. eugenioides</i> by running Stacks with the option –r (minimum percentage of individuals in a population required to process a locus for that population) set to 0.50 and the option–max-clipped (maximum soft-clipping level, in fraction of read length) set to the value of 0.68, corresponding to a minimum stack length of 30 bp. For these analyses, sites in which all <i>C. eugenioides</i> accessions were homozygous reference and all <i>C. canephora</i> accessions were homozygous alternative, or vice versa, were interpreted as residual homoeologous SNPs and filtered out, as well as sites within missing data in <i>C. eugenioides</i> and <i>C. canephora</i> and &gt;95% heterozygous calls in <i>C. arabica</i>. Nucleotide diversity and Tajima’s <i>D</i> were calculated for each group using the diversity.stats method included in the R package PopGenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Pfeifer, B., Wittelsbu, U., Ramos-onsins, S. E. &amp; Lercher, M. J. PopGenome: An Efficient Swiss Army Knife for Population Genomic Analyses in R. Mol Biol Evol. 31, 1929–36 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-61216-7#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d29985e2224">75</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The data have been deposited with links to BioProject accession number PRJNA554647 in the NCBI BioProject database (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/</a>). The genome sequence is available at <a href="https://worldcoffeeresearch.org/work/coffea-arabica-genome/">https://worldcoffeeresearch.org/work/coffea-arabica-genome/</a>.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 11 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=The%20coffee%20genome%20provides%20insight%20into%20the%20convergent%20evolution%20of%20caffeine%20biosynthesis.%20Science%20%2880-%29&amp;journal=Am.%20Assoc.%20Advancement%20Sci.&amp;volume=345&amp;pages=1181-4&amp;publication_year=2014&amp;author=Denoeud%2CF">
                    Google Scholar</a> 
                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="headline" content="Tran, H.                         class="js-separator">, </span><span>Yanhua Li</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Hualei Huang</span></li><li><span

                        class="js-separator">, </span><span>Jiana Li</span></li><li><span
                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Kun Lu</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-5">
                <i>Genomics</i>
                (2020)

      </div>
